    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13314336286650951481">Considered by Rumi to be “the master” of Sufi mystic poetry, Attar is best known for this epic poem, a magnificent allegorical tale about the soul’s search for meaning. He recounts the perilous journey of the world’s birds to the faraway peaks of Mount Qaf in search of the mysterious Simorgh, their king. Attar’s beguiling anecdotes and humor intermingle the sublime with th</span>
  <span id="freeText13314336286650951481" style="display:none">Considered by Rumi to be “the master” of Sufi mystic poetry, Attar is best known for this epic poem, a magnificent allegorical tale about the soul’s search for meaning. He recounts the perilous journey of the world’s birds to the faraway peaks of Mount Qaf in search of the mysterious Simorgh, their king. Attar’s beguiling anecdotes and humor intermingle the sublime with the mundane, the spiritual with the worldly, while his poem models the soul’s escape from the mind’s rational embrace.<br /><br /><br />Sholeh Wolpé re-creates for modern readers the beauty and timeless wisdom of the original Persian, in contemporary English verse and poetic prose.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13314336286650951481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15700845836257170474"><b>منطق الطیر - هفت شهر عشق = Mantiq Al-Tayr = Maqāmāt-uṭ-Ṭuyūr = The Conference of the Birds, Farid ud-Din Attar </b><br/><br/>The Conference of the Birds or Speech of the Birds (1177), is a celebrated literary masterpiece of Persian literature by poet Farid ud-Din Attar, commonly known as Attar of Nishapur. In the poem, the birds of the world gather to decide who is to be their sovereign, as they have none. The hoopoe, the wisest of them all, suggests that they should find the legendary Simorgh. The hoopoe lea</span>
  <span id="freeText15700845836257170474" style="display:none"><b>منطق الطیر - هفت شهر عشق = Mantiq Al-Tayr = Maqāmāt-uṭ-Ṭuyūr = The Conference of the Birds, Farid ud-Din Attar </b><br /><br />The Conference of the Birds or Speech of the Birds (1177), is a celebrated literary masterpiece of Persian literature by poet Farid ud-Din Attar, commonly known as Attar of Nishapur. In the poem, the birds of the world gather to decide who is to be their sovereign, as they have none. The hoopoe, the wisest of them all, suggests that they should find the legendary Simorgh. The hoopoe leads the birds, each of whom represent a human fault which prevents human kind from attaining enlightenment.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 1973میلادی، تاریخ خوانش این نسخه؛ ماه ژوئن سال 1988میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: منطق الطیر - هفت شهر عشق؛ نویسنده: شیخ فریدالدین محمد بن ابراهیم عطار نیشاپوری؛ تلخیض، مقدمه، و شرح حسین محی الدین الهی قمشه ای؛ تهران، علمی فرهنگی، 1373؛ در 11ص و 248ص؛ شابک 9644451651؛ چاپ سوم 1379؛ چهارم 1380؛ پنجم 1382؛ نهم 1391؛ شابک 9789644451652؛ موضوع شعر شاعران ایرانی سده ششم هجری - سده 12م<br /><br />عنوان: داستان منطق الطیر (هفت شهر عشق)؛ نویسنده: شیخ فریدالدین محمد بن ابراهیم عطار نیشاپوری؛ تلخیض، شرح لغات و عبارات حسین حداد؛ تهران، قدیانی، 1376؛ در 396ص؛ شابک 9644171551؛ چاپ دوم 1379؛ چهارم 1387؛ <br /><br /><b>پیشنهاد میکنم نگاره ام را تا پایان بخوانید</b><br /><br />داستان سفر گروهی از مرغان به راهنمایی «هدهد» است، که به کوه «قاف»، رهسپار هستند، برای دیدار «سیمرغ»، تنها موجودیکه راز «مرگ» و «زندگی» داند، و پزشک دردهای ناشناخته نیز هست؛<br /><br />هفت وادی در پیش است: «وادی طلب»، «وادی عشق»، «وادی معرفت»؛، «وادی استغنا»، «وادی توحيد»، «وادی حيرت»، و «وادی فقروفنا»؛<br /><br />وادی نخست «طلب» است؛ چون فرود آیی به وادی طلب، پیشت آید هر زمانی صد تعب؛<br /><br />وادی دوم «عشق» است؛ عطار عشق را مبتنی بر طلب، و طلب را مبتنی بر شناخت میدانستند؛ «هدهد» سالکان را از راه و مقصد آگاه میکند، یزرگواری معشوق، شوری در دلها میافکند، و سالکان را میجنباند؛ سالک شیدا، مسیر عشق را با پای دل، میپیماید؛ <br /><br />چون وادی عشق پیموده شد، وادی سوم که «معرفت» است، از راه میرسد، در وادی عشق، شناخت اجمالی (کلی)، و به وادی معرفت، گسترش یافته است، در این وادی، شهود بذر معرفت (دانایی) را آبیاری میکند، معرفت حضور در جان سالک، ریشه میدواند، سالک با اسماء، و صفات حق تعالی، در این وادی بیشتر آشنا میشود، گاه بعض اسماء و صفات در او به فعلیت میرسد، سالک اسرار بسیار درمییابد؛ سر ذراتش، همه، روشن شود، گلخن دنیا، بر او، گلشن شود، در این مرحله خوارق عادات از سالک سر میزند، میبیند که: جمله عالم، یک فروغ روی اوست؛<br /><br />از همگان امید میبرد، و به وادی «استغنا (بی نیازی)» گام میگذارد؛ آنگاه خوارق عادات، کنار میگذارد و کتمان سیر، پیشه میکند؛ پس ازین وادی «استغنا» بود، نه در او دعوی و نه معنی بود؛ میجهد از بی نیازی صرصری، میزند برهم به یکدم کشوری؛ سالک در این وادی بی نیازی حق، و نیاز مردم به حق الیقین را، شاهد میشود؛<br /><br />به وادی «توحید» میرسد، توحید ذاتی و صفاتی و افعالی را درک میکند؛ بعد ازین وادی توحید آیدت، منزل تفرید (دانا شدن) و تجرید (تنهایی) آیدت<br /><br />سپس به وادی «حیرت» میرسد، که شیرینتر از هزار بیداری ست. مات و مبهوت (شگفت زده) است، و میماند<br /><br />آنگاه به منزل «فقر و فنا» راهش میدهند؛ فانی در حق شده، باقی در او، میماند<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 19/08/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_35088555" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35088555" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35088555?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="pdf " href="/book/show/1559771._">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35088555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5318209430772516331">Manṭiq-uṭ-Ṭayr = Maqāmāt-uṭ-Ṭuyūr = The Conference of the Birds = Speech of the Birds, Farid ud-Din Attar<br/>The Conference of the Birds or Speech of the Birds (Manṭiq-uṭ-Ṭayr, also known as Maqāmāt-uṭ-Ṭuyūr; 1177), is a celebrated literary masterpiece of Persian literature by poet Farid ud-Din Attar, commonly known as Attar of Nishapur. In the poem, the birds of the world gather to decide who is to be their sovereign, as they have none. The hoopoe, the wisest of them all, suggests that they should </span>
  <span id="freeText5318209430772516331" style="display:none">Manṭiq-uṭ-Ṭayr = Maqāmāt-uṭ-Ṭuyūr = The Conference of the Birds = Speech of the Birds, Farid ud-Din Attar<br />The Conference of the Birds or Speech of the Birds (Manṭiq-uṭ-Ṭayr, also known as Maqāmāt-uṭ-Ṭuyūr; 1177), is a celebrated literary masterpiece of Persian literature by poet Farid ud-Din Attar, commonly known as Attar of Nishapur. In the poem, the birds of the world gather to decide who is to be their sovereign, as they have none. The hoopoe, the wisest of them all, suggests that they should find the legendary Simorgh. The hoopoe leads the birds, each of whom represent a human fault which prevents human kind from attaining enlightenment. The hoopoe tells the birds that they have to cross seven valleys in order to reach the abode of Simorgh. These valleys are as follows:<br />1. Valley of the Quest, where the Wayfarer begins by casting aside all dogma, belief, and unbelief.<br />2. Valley of Love, where reason is abandoned for the sake of love.<br />3. Valley of Knowledge, where worldly knowledge becomes utterly useless.<br />4. Valley of Detachment, where all desires and attachments to the world are given up. Here, what is assumed to be “reality” vanishes.<br />5. Valley of Unity, where the Wayfarer realizes that everything is connected and that the Beloved is beyond everything, including harmony, multiplicity, and eternity.<br />6. Valley of Wonderment, where, entranced by the beauty of the Beloved, the Wayfarer becomes perplexed and, steeped in awe, finds that he or she has never known or understood anything.<br />7. Valley of Poverty and Annihilation, where the self disappears into the universe and the Wayfarer becomes timeless, existing in both the past and the future.<br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 1973 میلادی تاریخ خوانش این نسخه ماه ژوئن سال 1988 میلادی <br />منطق الطیر جناب عطار نیشاپور، گفتگو و تدارک سفر مرغان است، که میخواهند به سیمرغ خویش برسند، و از ایشان و از خویش بپرسند. حضرت مولوی، عطار را روح خود لقب میداده است. گفته ی ایشان است که: «هفت شهر عشق را عطار گشت / ما هنوز اندر خم یک کوچه ایم». داستان سفر گروهی از مرغان به راهنمایی هدهد، به کوه قاف، برای دیدار سیمرغ ست، تنها موجودی که راز مرگ و زندگی داند، و پزشک دردهای ناشناخته است. هفت وادی در پیش مرغانست: وادی طلب، وادی عشق، وادی معرفت، وادی استغنا، وادی توحيد، وادی حيرت، و وادی فقروفنا. وادی نخست: طلب است. «چون فرود آیی به وادی طلب، پیشت آید هر زمانی صد تعب». وادی دوم: وادی عشق است. عطار عشق را مبتنی بر طلب، و طلب را مبتنی بر شناخت میداند. هدهد سالکان را از راه و مقصد آگاه میکند، عظمت معشوق شوری در دلها میافکند، و سالکان را میجنباند. سالک شیدا، مسیر عشق را با پای دل، میپیماید. چون وادی عشق پیموده شد. وادی سوم: که وادی معرفت است، از راه میرسد. در وادی عشق، شناخت اجمالی، و در وادی معرفت، تفضیلی است. در این وادی، شهود، بذر معرفت را، آبیاری میکند. معرفت حضور، در جان سالک ریشه میدواند. سالک با اسماء، و صفات حق تعالی، در این وادی بیشتر آشنا میشود. گاه بعض اسماء و صفات، در او به فعلیت میرسند. سالک اسرار درمییابد. «سِرّ ذراتش، همه روشن شود، گلخن دنیا، بر او گلشن شود». در این مرحله خوارق عادات از سالک سر میزند. میبیند که: «جمله عالم یک فروغ روی اوست». از همه امید میبرد، و به وادی استغنا گام میگذارد. آنگاه خوارق عادات، کنار میگذارد، و کتمان سِرّ پیشه میکند. «بعد ازین وادی استغنا بود، نه در او دعوی و نه معنی بود. میجهد از بی نیازی صرصری، میزند برهم به یکدم کشوری». سالک در این وادی بی نیازی حق، و نیاز مردم به حق الیقین را شاهد میشود. سپس به وادی توحید میرسد، توحید ذاتی، و صفاتی، و افعالی را، درک میکند. «بعد ازین وادی توحید آیدت، منزل تفرید و تجرید آیدت». پس از آن به وادی حیرت میرسد، شیرینتر از هزار بیداری. مات و مبهوت است و میماند. آنگاه است که به منزل فقر و فنا راهش میدهند. فانی در حق شده، باقی در او، میماند. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="5318209430772516331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1268136365" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1268136365" />
    <a title="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15726428-reemk10-paper-pills"><img alt="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507553429p2/15726428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1268136365?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" href="/user/show/15726428-reemk10-paper-pills">ReemK10 (Paper Pills)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781566564809" href="/book/show/225522.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1268136365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3864238578190834028">￼<br/><br/>I have been wanting to read The Conference of the Birds for a very long time. The peacock in me ordered the Raficq Abdulla interpretation, lured as I was by the illustrations of Persian miniatures from The British Library (this is the only, modern illustrated edition), and they do not disappoint.<br/><br/>￼<br/><br/>This is only a 93-page book. It serves as a delightful amuse-bouche but leaves one with a hungry appetite for more. This can be found in Peter Avery's The Speech of the Birds which the owl in me will b</span>
  <span id="freeText3864238578190834028" style="display:none">￼<br /><br />I have been wanting to read The Conference of the Birds for a very long time. The peacock in me ordered the Raficq Abdulla interpretation, lured as I was by the illustrations of Persian miniatures from The British Library (this is the only, modern illustrated edition), and they do not disappoint.<br /><br />￼<br /><br />This is only a 93-page book. It serves as a delightful amuse-bouche but leaves one with a hungry appetite for more. This can be found in Peter Avery's The Speech of the Birds which the owl in me will be reading next as it is the more scholarly translation and will hopefully have more clues. <br /><br />￼<br /><br />What I love, truly, deeply, passionately, emphatically love about Farid ad-Din Attar's masterpiece is coming across allegories that bring about enlightenment. Ah sublime bliss! But as always when you deal with metaphor and rhetorical riddles, you need to come prepared with a heavily-built superstructure of previously acquired knowledge beforehand to be able to correctly make your own connections and understandings of this poem. You need to have lived! The more you know, the more you will understand, and hence the constant urge to keep reading and learning!<br /><br />￼<br /><br />I think that the birds led by the hoopoe are prefect parables of us as readers who are often unbearably self-aware.<br /><br />￼<br /><br />The ishq of Sufism seduces us into squeezing meaning out of every word in this very intimate mystic poem. Attar absolutely delights as he takes us on this journey of life: love, understanding, detachment, unity, bewilderment, deprivation and death and our own psychological and spiritual journey as we deal with our personal flaws and disappointments.<br /><br />￼<br /><br />Abdalla makes this ancient masterpiece of a poem very accessible to the modern reader.<br /><br />￼<br /><br />"Nothing I know,<br />I understand nothing, I am surface-dead<br />Only loves survives, I am traveling unsurely, I go<br />To the Beloved unknown to this heart waiting to be read."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3864238578190834028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText842482947284913083" style="display:none">These poems about, the birds of the world gather to decide who is to be their king, as they have none. The hoopoe, the wisest of them all, suggests that they should find the legendary Simorgh, a mythical persian bird roughly equivalent to the western phoenix. It is an allegory of the quest for God (The Simorgh). The hoopoe respresents a sufi master and each of the other birds represents a human fault which prevents man from attaining enlightenment. When the group of thirty birds finally reach the dwelling place of the Simorgh, all they find is a lake in which they see their own reflection.<br /></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2020068571" />
    <a title="Biblio Curious" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26626256-biblio-curious"><img alt="Biblio Curious" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546381219p2/26626256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2020068571?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Biblio Curious" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Biblio Curious" href="/user/show/26626256-biblio-curious">Biblio Curious</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26626256-biblio-curious?shelf=classics-middle-east">classics-middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26626256-biblio-curious?shelf=drama-poems">drama-poems</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2020068571" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5112783855704359900">Amazing, mystical, philosophical, & anti-philosophical all wrapped into beautifully translated couplets.<br/><br/>Dick Davis stayed as true to the original format as possible. This edition includes the previously untranslated Prologue & Epilogue. The "Note on Translation" says the original Persian poem has rhyming couplets with a similar meter as the English "Heroic Couplet" of twenty syllables. The flow of Davis' translation lets the story sing, his rhyme scheme is never clunky and actually enhances the </span>
  <span id="freeText5112783855704359900" style="display:none">Amazing, mystical, philosophical, &amp; anti-philosophical all wrapped into beautifully translated couplets.<br /><br />Dick Davis stayed as true to the original format as possible. This edition includes the previously untranslated Prologue &amp; Epilogue. The "Note on Translation" says the original Persian poem has rhyming couplets with a similar meter as the English "Heroic Couplet" of twenty syllables. The flow of Davis' translation lets the story sing, his rhyme scheme is never clunky and actually enhances the pairing of the couplets.<br /><br />From the Prologue:<br /><br />"Your soul will be the talisman then bidden<br />To be the body bearing what is hidden."<br /> (Lines 139-158)<br /><br />The story itself is essentially an allegory of birds being compared to human nature. They go on a quest to find the Simorgh; a mystical King to become their leader. It's actually a potpourri of many little parables, problems told in story snippets that are replied with scenes of wisdom. Overall, it's told from both a very macro &amp; micro point of view. <br /><br />There are many references to the earliest books of the Koran/Torah/Bible. I'd recommend this great book to folks of all 3 faiths because there is some solid common ground for everyone. I'd also recommend it for people who enjoy philosophy about universal concepts or folks who enjoy a good story about bygone days.<br /><br />It's my first real experience with Persian poetry &amp; certainly will not be my last.<br /><br />Here's a little quote video I made for this amazing book (spoiler free &amp; the best parts are left out of course)<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://youtu.be/wwQbMXrg6AI">https://youtu.be/wwQbMXrg6AI</a><br /><br />My finished review on my blog:<br />Conference of the Birds: Allegory for Humanity<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.biblioatlas.com/2019/04/conference-of-birds.html">http://www.biblioatlas.com/2019/04/co...</a></span>

<div id="review_721893684" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/721893684" />
    <a title="Qais Omar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22441656-qais-omar"><img alt="Qais Omar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442637973p2/22441656.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/721893684?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Qais Omar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Qais Omar" href="/user/show/22441656-qais-omar">Qais Omar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer721893684" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2572304748687648425">The premise of Farid ud-Din Attar's poems in The Conference of the Birds is simple: the birds gather to seek the king of the birds, the Simorgh. One of the birds, the hoopoe, tells them that the Simorgh lives far away and that the journey there is hazardous. <br/><br/>First, the birds are enthusiastic to begin their search for the Simorgh -- a metaphor for Almighty God in Sufi mysticism -- but when they realize how difficult the journey will be, they start to make excuses. For instance, the finch says tha</span>
  <span id="freeText2572304748687648425" style="display:none">The premise of Farid ud-Din Attar's poems in The Conference of the Birds is simple: the birds gather to seek the king of the birds, the Simorgh. One of the birds, the hoopoe, tells them that the Simorgh lives far away and that the journey there is hazardous. <br /><br />First, the birds are enthusiastic to begin their search for the Simorgh -- a metaphor for Almighty God in Sufi mysticism -- but when they realize how difficult the journey will be, they start to make excuses. For instance, the finch says that he is too weak and cowardly. The hawk says he is satisfied with his position at the court waiting on the kings. The nightingale says he cannot leave his beloved the rose, and so on. The birds are identified by their species, and each species indicates a human type, and their excuses for not going on the journey are given according to their kind. <br /><br />The hoopoe, on the other hand, represents the birds’ guide, and he is the equivalent of a Sheikh in Sufism leading a group of Sufis. He answers each of the bird’s excuses with anecdotes, and each anecdote shows how their desires and fears are mistaken. <br /><br />Finally, the group formally accepts the hoopoe as a leader. They fly a little, and then before proceeding further, they ask him a series of questions about the journey. Again, the hoopoe answers them with anecdotes. Each answer contains two or three stories, illustrating the particular point the hoopoe is making. All the stories are linked together by admonition and commentary. <br /><br />The last questions are about the length of the journey, on which they must cross seven valleys. Why "seven" valleys? The number "seven" in Islam is significant because in the Koran it is the most repeated number after the number "One", which represents God. Seventh heaven, above which is the throne of God. Seven doorways to paradise, which are represented by the seven valleys in The Conference of the Birds, through which the birds must pass to finally reach God. The seventh day for ‘Aqeeqa’, one of the four stages in Sufism. Seven stages for the completion of man’s creation....<br /><br />In The Conference of the Birds, Attar frequently stresses one doctrine: the soul is trapped within the cage of the body. It can reach God if it wishes, but to unite with God, the soul must look inward and make an internal journey to pass the seven valleys to reach God.<br /><br />The journey is quickly completed. The birds reach the court of the Simorgh. But when they first arrive, they are turned back. Finally, they understand that the Simorgh they have sought is none other than themselves, and that they have completed their internal journey. <br /><br />The Conference of the Birds is beautifully written, and I highly recommend it. <br /><br />Qais Akbar Omar</span>
  <a data-text-id="2572304748687648425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1638758022" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1638758022" />
    <a title="Christopher Porzenheim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55881969-christopher-porzenheim"><img alt="Christopher Porzenheim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486182224p2/55881969.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1638758022?book_show_action=true">May 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher Porzenheim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher Porzenheim" href="/user/show/55881969-christopher-porzenheim">Christopher Porzenheim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Someone willing to apply a (slow-er) story to oneself</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Christopher by: </span>
      My curiosity
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55881969-christopher-porzenheim?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55881969-christopher-porzenheim?shelf=persian-literature">persian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55881969-christopher-porzenheim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55881969-christopher-porzenheim?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55881969-christopher-porzenheim?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1638758022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7647465695364673143"><i>The Conference of the Birds</i> is a Sufi allegorical story written by Attar about multiple birds quest to find God.<br/><br/>Each bird represents a certain type of person with certain virtues or vices. The birds are greedy, lazy, fearful, arrogant, and so on.<br/><br/>Attar's poem is a mirror which we can use to see parts of ourselves and others.<br/><br/>Attar's hope was that his birds quest could serve as a mirror we can use to become more self aware. Here is Attar's verse at the poems conclusion:  <br/><br/><i>I, with my words,<br/>Have shown</i></span>
  <span id="freeText7647465695364673143" style="display:none"><i>The Conference of the Birds</i> is a Sufi allegorical story written by Attar about multiple birds quest to find God.<br /><br />Each bird represents a certain type of person with certain virtues or vices. The birds are greedy, lazy, fearful, arrogant, and so on.<br /><br />Attar's poem is a mirror which we can use to see parts of ourselves and others.<br /><br />Attar's hope was that his birds quest could serve as a mirror we can use to become more self aware. Here is Attar's verse at the poems conclusion:  <br /><br /><i>I, with my words,<br />Have shown to sleeping men their souls as birds.<br />And if these words can prompt one heart to wake<br />From lifelong stupor for their mystery’s sake,<br />I’ll know, I’ll have no doubt, that all my pain<br />And grief are over, and were not in vain-<br />I will have been a lamp, a candle’s light<br />That burns itself, and makes the whole world bright.</i><br /><br />Attar's poem is a mirror for cultivating self awareness.<br /><br />Attar's poem begins with the birds electing the Hoopoe as their guide. As soon as the journey begins so do the birds excuses, doubts, and questions. The Hoopoe answers each bird with 3-4 allegorical stories. This pattern of question and multiple stories as an answer repeats and makes up most of <i>the Conference of the Birds.</i> <br /><br />For example, one bird fears death. So the Hoopoe tells a story about Socrates:<br /><br /><i>When Socrates lay close to death, a youth-<br />Who was his student in the search for Truth-<br />Said: ‘Master, when we’ve washed the man we knew<br />And brought your shroud, where should we bury you?'<br />He said: 'If you can find me when I’ve died,<br />Then bury me wherever you decide.-<br />I’ve never found myself; I cannot see<br />How when I’m dead you could discover me.<br />Throughout my life not one small particle<br />Had any knowledge of itself at all!'</i><br /><br />Sounds like the ever whimsical Socrates to me.<br /><br />Attar mimics and, I think, surpasses Plato's story about this same moment: <br /><br /><i>[Crito said] 'how shall we bury you?' 'In any way you like,' said Socrates, 'if you can catch me and I do not escape you.' And laughing quietly, looking at us, he said: 'I do not convince Crito that I am this Socrates talking to you here and ordering all I say, but he thinks that I am the thing which he will soon be looking at as a corpse, and so he asks how he shall bury me.'</i> (Plato's Phaedo at 115c-116a.)<br /><br />Platonic echo aside, Attar's Conference of Bird's also resembles Dante's Inferno; both stories are framed as journeys to God which explore human virtues and vices for listener and or readers benefit.<br /><br />Like Dante, Attar had a soft spot for the virtuous of other religious traditions. As we've seen for example, noble non-Muslims such as Socrates are approved of by Attar.<br /><br />But despite this soft spot for specific virtuous Greek philosophers, such as as Socrates, Attar condemns Greek philosophy in general:<br /><br /><i>How will you know the truths religion speaks<br />While your’re philosophizing with the Greeks?<br />How can you be a man of faith while you’re still wrapped in their philosophic lore?<br />If someone traveling on love’s Way should say<br />'Philosophy', he doesn’t know loves way; [...]<br />Philosophy, though, snares you with its ‘why’s<br />And ‘how’s, and it mostly ensnares the wise. [...]<br />Medina’s wisdom is enough, my friend,<br />Throw dirt on Greece, and all that Greece might send.</i><br /><br />Attar sees that love is the true path to wisdom and God, and that Greek philosophy leads us away from love.<br /><br />I have to disagree with Attar.<br /><br />Generally, Greek philosophers saw love and the proper practice of it as the key to self awareness and self improvement. Take Plato for example, he thought that the sight of beauty quickens love which through the use of reason leads to the truth. Philosophy, is after all, a love of wisdom.<br /><br />Generally speaking, proper reason mirrors proper love in the Greek philosophical tradition; one cannot reason well without love or love well without reason; reason and love act in harmony when either is done well.<br /><br />But Attar, unlike his Greek predecessors, sees reason as always in opposition to love. As he puts it so eloquently:<br /><br /><i>My book’s all madness, Reason won’t appear<br />Within it’s pages, she’s a stranger here,<br />And till the soul breathes in this madness she<br />Remains a stranger to eternity.</i><br /><br />I've spent too much time philosophizing with the Greeks to be able to agree with Attar's complete rejection of reason.<br /><br />But, thankfully, we don't need to agree with Attar entirely to see how we can use his poem as a mirror to ritually channel our awareness for the sake of self improvement.<br /><br />We simply need to be very clear about why Attar distrusts reason...<br /><br />So, what's the reason Attar distrusts 'reason'?<br /><br />I think we all can benefit from taking Attar seriously here, especially if you're the kind of person attached to seeing themselves as 'logical' or 'rational' rather than 'emotional.'<br /><br />It would be easy, boring and lazy to dismiss Attar's distrust of reason as complete nonsense. More to the point, such a lazy dismissal would make it much harder to enjoy or benefit from Attar's Conference of Bird's.<br /><br />Whether or not we agree with Attar's reasons for distrusting reason we need to understand them to understand him, or give a real reason why he might be wrong.<br /><br />Here's what I think Attar is up to...<br /><br />Now, I'm certain you've noticed at some point in your life that you can rarely -if ever- convince people to believe something based on pure logic, reason, or empirical claims.<br /><br />That's because a more reliable way -perhaps the only reliable way- to change someone's mind is telling them stories that appeal indirectly to them.<br /><br />This is of course, exactly what Attar does.<br /><br />Attar's poem is a bunch of stories, not a bunch of systematic 'reasons.'<br /><br />Still, there is a recurring pattern behind most of Attar's enjoyable stories, one which amounts to a kind of reasoned argument when we examine his poem as a whole.<br /><br />In Attar's stories, usually a lover falls in love with someone they are perceived as being unworthy to love: a beggar for a prince, an ascetic for a princess, a Sufi for God, etc,<br /><br />Generally, the pattern in Attar's poem is that a social inferior falls in love with a social superior. This love is usually seen as scandalous, and as a rule of thumb the lovers love for the beloved is questioned and challenged by society. <br /><br />Most of Attar's stories end badly for the social inferior. Often in death.<br /><br />But this 'death' is shown as a metaphor for our egos death and changes in our self understanding. Death is seen as only a kind of change, and change as a kind of death.<br /><br />This is why Attar's and Plato's Socrates laughs when his followers desire to bury 'him.' 'Socrates' won't be around after death. Nor will 'Socrates' experience his own death. In other words, death is not an event 'we' experience in life.<br /><br />There's some wisdom in distrusting reason in favor of intuitive love.<br /><br />For it is only when we let go of who we think we are -and what we are attached to thinking we look like to others- that we can become more aware of who we truly are, which is the first step in any process of self improvement.<br /><br />The problem with Attar, as I see it, is that I doubt he would be satisfied with this conclusion, he would want more of us than 'just' cultivating our self awareness for the sake of self and societal improvement.<br /><br />Attar would want us to go even further, to dissolve our Self and loyalty to others into a mystic rapture of 'oneness' with the world; by submitting to a true and un-reasoned intuitive and spontaneous love for God, the universe, and everyone in it.<br /><br />Here's why I think Attar would want us to go one step farther. As the translators of my <i>the Conference of Birds</i>, Dick Davis and Afkham Darbani, put it in their introduction:<br /><br /><i>Certain of the beliefs central to Sufism seem to engage Attar’s imagination more than others. Two themes in particular are diffused throughout the whole poem -the necessity for destroying the Self, and the importance of passionate love. Both are mentioned in every conceivable context and not only at the ‘appropriate’ moments in the narrative. The two [themes] are connected: the Self is seen as an entity dependent on pride and reputation, there can be no progress until the pilgrim is indifferent to both, and the surest way of making him indifferent is the experience of overwhelming love.</i><br /><br />Attar's stories show again, and again, and again, that through the right kind of love we over come our selfish vanity and find the nature of god, the universe, and ourselves as more interrelated than we imagined.<br /><br />Attar wishes us to see that we are the universe witnessing itself -that we are all 'god'- and that the right kind of love is indiscriminate, universal, and impartial to all 'differences.' After all, if 'we' are 'everything' and all 'god' then preferring to love some people or things more than others is downright foolish, because all we are doing is rejecting 'ourselves.'<br /><br />This is why the <i>Conference of the Birds</i> ends the way it does.<br /><br />At the very end of the story, the birds that persevered throughout the poem and heard all of the Hoopoes stories find themselves looking into a pond. Within it, they see God within themselves reflected, the very thing they started their journey in search of.<br /><br />Attar's prologue succinctly states what we are to take away from that scene and <i>the Conference of the Birds</i> as a whole:<br /><br /><i>The things you seek and know are you, and so<br />It’s you a hundred ways, you're forced to know.</i><br /><br />I think this is the reason why Attar distrusts reason in general.<br /><br />Reasoning with others always creates conceptual differences between things, otherwise we can't speak of one thing being different than another. And if we are the universe witnessing itself, then 'reason,' as well as language, merely creates false categories and abstractions, enslaving us to concepts, blinding us to how interconnected everything and everyone is.<br /><br />While I think this is all a fine metaphor we benefit from learning, I don't think it's literally true and I also think this metaphor creates just as many problems as it solves.<br /><br />Let me first explain where I think Attar is right, and helpful to us all, before I move on to places where he creates problems for us if we value social relationships, friendship, or family.<br /><br />We can all agree that I am not the same person I was when I was 10.<br /><br />The only reason I think of there being a continuity between 10 year old me and present day me is because I automatically use the concept of the Self. <br /><br />As I imagine Attar might wisely say: I am a fool if I out of loyalty to 10 year old me, I refuse to try a new activity because I want to be 'true to myself.'<br /><br />For example, 10 year old me thought that skateboarding was something dumb punks did because 10 year old me recited the opinions of his Catholic grandma. Spongebob Squarepants was also suspect, for her, and thus for me, just in case you were wondering. The early 2000s Kids Tv Show Rocket Power was also suspect in my childhood. Why? It glorified the great satan of skateboarding.<br /><br />Anyway, years later, I only realized how silly my suspicion of Spongebob and skateboarding was as friends of mine told me stories about their love of Spongebob and skateboarding.<br /><br />These friends opened up new (and truer) ways of seeing the world for me.<br /><br />My reasons to distrust Spongebob and skateboarding were ridiculous, but if someone had tried to reason or argue me out of them, I likely wouldn't have listened. My 'reasons' came from a foolish, if understandable, loyalty to the opinions of a loved one.<br /><br />Attar is, like a good friend, excellent at telling us stories which help us get rid of silly ideas we get from ourselves and others about who we really are, can be, and whether Spongebob is corrupting the youth.<br /><br />Sometimes 'reason' is just a convenient and socially respected cudgel we -or society- use on ourselves and others as a means of creating a conformity as meaningless as it is misguided or damaging. This is Attar's concern, and why he can help us.<br /><br />Attar is great at helping us see and imagine who we really are, and could become, because his stories constantly remind us how fluid who we are really is.<br /><br />So that's what sound about Attar's distrust of reason, and why he's especially worth a read, but here's the rub.<br /><br />Attar doesn't distrust reason in specific cases; he doubts reason is ever useful. Because if we are the universe witnessing itself, reason can only ever divide and blind us into partial, foolish, and selfish love.<br /><br />From Attar's way of seeing things, my 10 year old preferential beliefs about skateboarding and Spongebob are as delusional as my preferential love for close friends and family.<br /><br />After all, where do we usually get get our reasons from, and who do we usually address them to? While we can marginally reason on our own, we primarily do so because of, with, and for others we have social relationships with.<br /><br />Reason is primarily a social tool, like storytelling, that we use to renew, repair, and reform our social relationships and society.<br /><br />This is why Attar's categorical distrust of reason leads to a categorical distrust all social relationships.<br /><br />From Attar's way of seeing things, both reason and social relationships imprison us into a partial, limited, and distinct sense of self apart from the universe as a whole -think of my reliance on my beloved Grandma's reasoning about skateboarding and spongebob.<br /><br />If you're like me, you probably don't find this side of Attar helpful, and want to pinpoint where he went wrong, especially because he's so helpful at freeing us from foolishly hating things, such as Spongebob.<br /><br />Plato can help us answer Attar.<br /><br />Plato's answer is one Attar might not agree with, but Plato's answer will let us read, enjoy, and make sense of Attar whether or not we fully agree with Attar's categorical distrust of reason.<br /><br />Plato has Socrates argue that a categorical distrust of reason is as foolish, misguided, and silly as hating all humanity.<br /><br /><i>SOCRATES: We must not become haters of reason as people become haters of humanity. There is no greater evil one can suffer than to hate reasonable discourse. A hatred of reason and a hatred of humanity arise in the same way.<br /><br />A hatred of humanity comes when someone without knowledge or skill has placed great trust in someone else and believes him to be altogether truthful, sound and trustworthy.<br /><br />Then a short time afterwards, they find him to be wicked and unreliable. Then this happens in another case. When this happens enough, especially with those you believed to be close friends, then in the end, after many such blows, people come to hate all humanity and to believe that no one is sound in any way at all.<br /><br />Such a hatred of humanity comes most easily to those that have little experience and skill in social relationships. For great experience would lead one to believe -what is in fact true- that the very good and the very wicked are both quite rare, and that most people are between these two extremes.</i> (A paraphrase of Plato's Phaedo at 89d-90a.)<br /> <br />A complete distrust of reason, verging on hatred, arises when we have frequently been let down by arguments. But this does not mean we should give up on our ability to talk clearly, truthfully, and honestly to each other.<br /><br />If we find ourselves constantly let down by others, or arguments, this means we have something to learn, not that we should give up on our ability to learn.<br /><br />Plato compared the hatred of reason to the hatred of humanity for a very good reason.<br /><br />For Plato, and much of the rest of Greek philosophy, reason was something you did by speaking with others, it was a social act, not a private mental one. Thus, to give up on reason did not mean giving up on your own ability to think or speak, but giving up on our collective ability to clearly discuss things, learn, and have plans and relationships with each other.<br /><br />Reason and love, on this view, are mean't to harmonize with each other, however exactly that harmony works out...<br /><br />I think, with Plato, that we should not hate everyone else, or all reason, but take courage, and eagerly question and wonder -in the company of trusted friends and storytellers- what led us to mistaken love or reason in the first place.  <br /><br />Still, whether we prefer to see love and reason like Plato or Attar, we can benefit from <i>the Conference of the Birds</i> by using it's stories as a mirror for cultivating self awareness. His poem, is like a journal, a way of regularly ritually reviewing the direction of our life.<br /><br />Attar's stories are enjoyable and useful with or without us fully agreeing with his views on love, reason, or God.<br /><br />Just please don't give up on reason or humanity because of a few bad apples.<br /><br />POSTSCRIPT: <a href="http://mirrorforprinces.net/bookreview/use-bird-stories-find-yourself/" rel="nofollow noopener">Click here for a rich text version of this review with pictures and links and footnotes.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="7647465695364673143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1396566487" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1396566487" />
    <a title="Hassan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14438724-hassan"><img alt="Hassan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1437593407p2/14438724.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1396566487?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hassan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hassan" href="/user/show/14438724-hassan">Hassan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14438724-hassan?shelf=epic">epic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14438724-hassan?shelf=sufism">sufism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14438724-hassan?shelf=persian-literature">persian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14438724-hassan?shelf=orient">orient</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14438724-hassan?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1396566487" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1602876538438178068">'The first stage is the Valley of the Quest;<br/>Then Love's wide valley is our second test;<br/>The third is Insight into Mystery,<br/>The fourth, Detachment and Serenity-<br/>The fifth is Unity; The sixth is Awe,<br/>A deep Bewilderment unknown before,<br/>The seventh Poverty and Nothingness-<br/>And there you are suspended,motionless,<br/>Till you are drawn- the impulse is not yours-<br/>A drop absorbed in seas that have no shores.'<br/><br/> I heard about this book when it was mentioned during a lecture by Wayne Dyer. He said that it was a fable</span>
  <span id="freeText1602876538438178068" style="display:none">'The first stage is the Valley of the Quest;<br />Then Love's wide valley is our second test;<br />The third is Insight into Mystery,<br />The fourth, Detachment and Serenity-<br />The fifth is Unity; The sixth is Awe,<br />A deep Bewilderment unknown before,<br />The seventh Poverty and Nothingness-<br />And there you are suspended,motionless,<br />Till you are drawn- the impulse is not yours-<br />A drop absorbed in seas that have no shores.'<br /><br /> I heard about this book when it was mentioned during a lecture by Wayne Dyer. He said that it was a fable about the birds who set forth to meet God but when they got to their destination after a violent journey, they found nothing but a mirror, reflecting who they had become during the journey. The knower and the known became one by virtue of that journey. That description intrigued me so I decided to read the book. Before I started reading, I was expecting the description of the journey to be the main chunk of the book. I was wrong. and I am glad that I was wrong. And the reason for not describing the journey in detail, Attar explains in the description of the valley of Bewilderment, is that each of the birds had to travel their own journey that is different from the rest. I saw Rumi in that explanation who once said,' Let the beauty we love be what we do. There are a hundred ways to kneel and kiss the ground.'Sure enough, I found during my research that Attar was a major inspiration for Rumi. Some even claim that Rumi met Attar but it is disputed. They might have not met physically, but you can see the spiritual union if you read both of them. <br /><br /> 'The Conference of the Birds' can be looked upon as an epic that accurately describes the Sufi tradition in South Asia. The birds (disciples) need a guide Hoopoe ( spiritual master) if they were to reach Simurgh (God) by traveling through seven valleys (Trials). According to the Sufi tradition, no one can reach God with out a teacher. That teacher holds unquestionable authority and the disciple has to obey everything, even if the instruction goes directly against the teachings of the faith. The spiritual masters teach that this is the only way to overcome ego or nafs as they call it in Persian. <br /><br /> Before the journey starts, the Hoopoe has to convince the other birds to make the journey. So he lets them pour out their deepest fears regarding the journey and then answers the inherent questions in those insecurities through fables. The symbolize another popular tradition is Sufism, that is teaching through fables and stories. The same tradition was found in the West, an example of whom is Canterbury Tales. In fact, there were stark similarities as well as differences between The Conference of The Birds and Canterbury Tales which might as well symbolize the similarities and differences between East and the West. The epic was a form of instruction in both East and The West but what was instructed is something very interesting to observe in both cases.<br /><br /> The end of the epic again is something very significant. It speaks of the presence of God in each of us. Access to that presence is denied to us because of the false self that we have created, that dictates us about who we are and who we are not. That false self or ego is the mirror reflection of the customs and traditions of the society. That ego has fears and subsequently the pride which is used to cover those fears. Most of the fables included in this book are directed towards showing the falsehood of these integral parts of the ego. The way to overcome these twin forces is something upon which both the East and the West agrees. Love. To be more precise from the Eastern side; the journey of love, towards love, with love. 'Love Conquers All' as Chaucer said. If I reflect upon my life, I find that what stops us from setting out on a journey is fear and pride in unequal proportions. But what annihilates that mixture is the force of love about whom Attar says, 'the impulse in not yours'. If the impulse is not ours, then I would say that the presence of God makes itself felt through that impulse. The end seems to signify that we don't reach our Beloved. We become that Beloved during our reaching. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1602876538438178068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1396566487">

<span id="freeTextContainer6915679632539180238">‘Mantiq-al-Tayr’ - The foundational epic twelfth century Sufi poem has immense doctrinal as well as literary value. <br/><br/>I have always had trouble reading longer verse but The Conference of the Birds flows very well. The initial part where the hoopoe is persuading the various birds to join in the pursuit for Simorgh is particularly delightful. The birds depict various facets and archetypes of human character and make their excuses or express reservations accordingly. The hoopoe adroitly takes apart t</span>
  <span id="freeText6915679632539180238" style="display:none">‘Mantiq-al-Tayr’ - The foundational epic twelfth century Sufi poem has immense doctrinal as well as literary value. <br /><br />I have always had trouble reading longer verse but The Conference of the Birds flows very well. The initial part where the hoopoe is persuading the various birds to join in the pursuit for Simorgh is particularly delightful. The birds depict various facets and archetypes of human character and make their excuses or express reservations accordingly. The hoopoe adroitly takes apart these excuses and reservations and explains that ultimate truth and pursuit of the same is what really matters and that everything else is transient and ephemeral. <br /><br />Khezr sought companionship with one whose mind <br />Was set on God alone. The man declined<br />And said to Khezr: "We two could not be friends,<br />For our existences have different ends.<br />The waters of immortal life are yours,<br />And you must always live; life is your cause<br />And death is mine - you wish to live, whilst I<br />Impatiently prepare myself to die;<br />I leave you as quick birds avoid a snare,<br />To soar up in the free, untrammeled air."<br /><br />"“You could not know <br />The hidden ways by which we lovers go;”<br /><br />"Give up the intellect for love and see <br />In one brief moment all eternity"<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1721707616" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1721707616" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1721707616?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=bestest">bestest</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=poems">poems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=myth-religion">myth-religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=2-asia">2-asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1721707616" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7952860158082185915"><i>”The Moths had long been exiled from the Flame<br/>They worship: so to solemn Council came,<br/>And voted One of them by Lot be sent<br/>To find their Idol. One was chosen: went. <br/>And after a long Circuit in sheer Gloom,<br/>Seeing, he thought, the TAPER in a Room<br/>Flew back at once to say so. But the chief<br/>Of Mothistan slighted so slight Belief,<br/>And sent another Messenger, who flew<br/>Up to the House, in at the window, through<br/>The Flame itself; and back the Message brings,<br/>With yet no sign of Conflict on his wings.<br/>Then went </i></span>
  <span id="freeText7952860158082185915" style="display:none"><i>”The Moths had long been exiled from the Flame<br />They worship: so to solemn Council came,<br />And voted One of them by Lot be sent<br />To find their Idol. One was chosen: went. <br />And after a long Circuit in sheer Gloom,<br />Seeing, he thought, the TAPER in a Room<br />Flew back at once to say so. But the chief<br />Of Mothistan slighted so slight Belief,<br />And sent another Messenger, who flew<br />Up to the House, in at the window, through<br />The Flame itself; and back the Message brings,<br />With yet no sign of Conflict on his wings.<br />Then went a Third, and spurr'd with true Desire,<br />Plunging at once into the sacred Fire, <br />Folded his Wings within, till he became<br />One Colour and one Substance with the Flame.<br /><b>He only knew the Flame who in it burn'd;<br />And only He could tell who ne'er to tell return'd. </b></i><br />(Parliament of Birds)<br /><br />Devotion and Love are such mass emotions that even if you frown at most of them, there will always be a few such poets for these emotions that you shall end up loving.<br /><br />Of all religious schools, I love Sufism the best. I find them really cool among all saints. They would spend their lives going around singing devotional songs. They are sort of rebels - breaking rules instead of making them. They are still really popular in Punjab and Pakistan – and are equally loved among Hindus, Muslims and Sikhs (Majority of population in on the planet is Christians, that of my country is Hindu, that of my state is Sikh and that of my town is Muslim and I’m an atheist.) Sufism is now an important branch of Islamic and Indian music. Nusrat Fateh Ali Khan was a popular singer.<br /><br />Sufism strives for ihsan or perfection of worship. The focus is on the emotion – your love for the God rather than the object iself – God. As to how perfect the emotion, the answer varies among different schools of Sufism. <br /><br />Still basic rule is that there are no rules – and even that is not a rule. Look at a poem from Buleh Shaw:<br /><br /><i> <br />“Not a believer in the mosque am I,<br />Nor a disbeliever with his rites am I.<br />I am not the pure amongst the impure,<br />I am neither Moses nor Pharaoh.<br />Bulleh, I know not who I am.<br /><br />Not in the holy books am I,<br />Nor do I dwell in bhang or wine,<br />Nor do I live in a drunken haze,<br />Nor in sleep or waking known.<br />Bulleh, I know not who I am.<br /><br />Not in happiness or in sorrow am I found.<br />I am neither pure nor mired in filthy ground.<br />Not of water nor of land,<br />Nor am I in air or fire to be found.<br />Bulleh, I know not who I am.<br /><br />Not an Arab nor Lahori,<br />Not a Hindi or Nagouri,<br />Nor a Muslim or Peshawari,<br />Not a Buddhist or a Christian.<br />Bulleh, I know not who I am.<br /><br />Secrets of religion have I not unravelled,<br />I am not of Eve and Adam.<br />Neither still nor moving on,<br />I have not chosen my own name!<br />Bulleh, I know not who I am.<br /><br />From first to last, I searched myself.<br />None other did I succeed in knowing.<br />Not some great thinker am I.<br />Who is standing in my shoes, alone?<br /><br />Bulleh, I know not who I am.” </i><br />(by Buleh Shah)<br /><br /><b><br />Poems and Parables<br /></b><br />Sufi saints won’t give you commandments but would rather write poems like lovers. They are very generous in challenging traditions of all religions (look at the Buleh Shaw poem) but they are equally generous in picking up their material from parables from those very same religions - and even love stories. Yes, they are not afraid of what all fanatics are afraid of – being romantic (no doubt they have been repeatedly under terrorist attacks in Pakistan). They will quote examples of Arabic Laila-Majnu and Indian Heer-Ranjha; both stories of stars-crossed lovers.<br /><br />There is actually this one Punjabi Sufi song where a saint, upon seeing a beautiful girl, would always go “oh my God! What a nice piece of work you have done.” It sounds more poetical when he says it. When asked, why wouldn't he worship the beauty he enjoys seeing so much; his reply is; “I don’t count the beads of my rosary. The beautiful faces are beads in my rosary. Devotion is automatically done as they pass through my sight."<br /><br />You see the metaphor of moth and flame above, another metaphor used in this poem is that Nightingale – who stands for lover singing for his beloved, Rose. When Nightingale asks waht does he need God for, when he has his rose. He is replied as follows:<br /><i><br />'O watering with thy melodious Tears<br />Love's Garden, and who dost indeed the Ears<br />Of men with thy melodious Fingers mould<br />As David's Finger Iron did of old:<br />Why not, like David, dedicate thy Dower<br />Of Song to something better than a Flower?<br />Empress indeed of Beauty, so they say, 230<br />But one whose Empire hardly lasts a Day,<br />By Insurrection of the Morning's Breath<br />That made her hurried to Decay and Death:<br />And while she lasts contented to be seen,<br />And worshipt, for the Garden's only Queen,<br />Leaving thee singing on thy Bough forlorn,<br />Or if she smile on Thee, perhaps in Scorn.'<br /><br />Like that fond Dervish waiting in the throng<br />When some World-famous Beauty went along,<br />Who smiling on the Antic as she pass'd—240<br />Forthwith Staff, Bead and Scrip away he cast,<br />And grovelling in the Kennel, took to whine<br />Before her Door among the Dogs and Swine.<br />Which when she often went unheeding by,<br />But one day quite as heedless ask'd him—'Why?'—<br />He told of that one Smile, which, all the Rest<br />Passing, had kindled Hope within his Breast—<br /><b>Again she smiled and said, 'O self-beguiled<br />Poor Wretch, at whom and not on whom I smiled.'</b><br /></i><br /><b><br />The Allegory<br /></b><br />Birds of all kind gather to search for Khalif-king for them. Tajinder the wise and our Sufi coach for the poem tells them that he know of their lord (read God) but they must journey out to see him:<br /><i><br />”Were you indeed not blinded by the Curse<br />Of Self-exile, that still grows worse and worse,<br />Yourselves would know that, though you see him not,<br />He is with you this Moment, on this Spot, 60<br />Your Lord through all Forgetfulness and Crime,<br />Here, There, and Everywhere, and through all Time.<br />But as a Father, whom some wayward Child<br />By sinful Self-will has unreconciled,<br />Waits till the sullen Reprobate at cost<br />Of long Repentance should regain the Lost;<br />Therefore, yourselves to see as you are seen,<br />Yourselves must bridge the Gulf you made between<br />By such a Search and Travel to be gone<br />Up to the mighty mountain Kaf, … <br /></i><br /><br />Different birds represent people of different nature. Nightingale is the lover; Peacock is vain about its beauty, Owl is the miser etc. Each of them have their doubts and are in turn answered by Tajinder or other birds by advice and through parables. I have already quoted Tajinder’s answer to Nightingale. It is circular - birds should use human parables, while we , humans are using them as `parables.<br /><br />The journey of birds is symbolic of human search for God:<br /><i><br />”Not to be reach'd, if to be reach'd at all<br />But by a Road the stoutest might apal;<br />Of Travel not of Days or Months, but Years—<br />Life-long perhaps: of Dangers, Doubts, and Fears<br />As yet unheard of: Sweat of Blood and Brain<br />Interminable—often all in vain—80<br />And, if successful, no Return again<br /></i><br />And<br /><i><br />”A voice from Heav'n amid the stillness said:<br />'The Royal Road is not for all to tread,<br />Nor is the Royal Palace for the Rout,<br />Who, even if they reach it, are shut out.<br />The Blaze that from my Harim window breaks<br />With fright the Rabble of the Roadside takes;<br />And even of those that at my Portal din,<br />Thousands may knock for one that enters in.”<br /></i><br /><br />Poet’s philosophy is that God is the greater whole of which we are parts ( and it is stupid to consider a part to be the whole) like rain drops are a part of sea. It is our destination to submerge/ submit ourselves to God (Islam means submission to God). Of course important difference here is that God does not go around giving us sets of rules. Submission here means giving up yourself aesthetically to God – selflessness to point of fault. Look at the moth and flame parable quoted in the beginning.<br /><br />And now, after a difficult journey only thirty birds dare able to reach their destination only to see in God their own reflection:<br /><i><br />They That, That They: Another, yet the Same:<br />Dividual, yet One<br /></i><br />Which again explains that God and people are same material:<br /><br /><i>”Rays that have wander'd into Darkness wide<br />Return, and back into your Sun subside.'</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7952860158082185915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2262367430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2262367430" />
    <a title="Matija" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71152033-matija"><img alt="Matija" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554663870p2/71152033.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2262367430?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matija" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matija" href="/user/show/71152033-matija">Matija</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71152033-matija?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2262367430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14265919419333614068">Attar’s Conference of the Birds is a masterpiece of Persian literature which had an immense influence on the eastern Islamic world. The central subject of this 4500-line philosophical poem is the soul’s search for meaning, veiled in an allegorical tale about the birds of the world who gather to decide who will be their king. Hoopoe, the wisest of the birds (sufis), is chosen as their leader and invites them to seek out their king (God), the mythical persian bird Simorgh, akin to the western Phoe</span>
  <span id="freeText14265919419333614068" style="display:none">Attar’s Conference of the Birds is a masterpiece of Persian literature which had an immense influence on the eastern Islamic world. The central subject of this 4500-line philosophical poem is the soul’s search for meaning, veiled in an allegorical tale about the birds of the world who gather to decide who will be their king. Hoopoe, the wisest of the birds (sufis), is chosen as their leader and invites them to seek out their king (God), the mythical persian bird Simorgh, akin to the western Phoenix and the slavic Firebird. <blockquote>
  <i>It was in China, late one moonless night,<br />The Simorgh first appeared to mortal sight --<br />He let a feather float down through the air,<br />And rumours of its fame spread everywhere;</i>
</blockquote>Most of the poem consists of hoopoe’s answers to the birds who offer exuses, in accordance with their kind, for being unable to endure the quest. These answers include quranic tales (a few of them are non-quranic, e.g. one is about the death of Socrates) – witty vignettes that serve as ethical and social allegories, which are often enigmatic and obscure. There are quite a few I didn’t understand at all.<br />After hoopoe insults about a dozen birds in an instructive manner, a hundred thousand of them set out on a quest through the seven valleys which symbolise the stages of human desires along with the qualities that one must attain to transition from these valleys. Only thirty birds manage to get through all seven and arrive in the land of Simorgh – there they find <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[an Iranian expendable small-capacity orbital carrier rocket. And a lake. I’m not going to spoil the rest, but if you decide to read this book, skip the introduction and return to it once you’ve finished with the poem. What they find is literally the "simorgh". <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This is a must-read for anyone interested in Sufism, Islam, or religion in general. It has made me see religion in an entirely new light. The edition I read (Penguin Classics, 1984) was translated by Dick Davis and Afkham Darbandi, and while it’s not the most accurate translation, it’s definitely the most poetic one. I skimmed through some other translations (Wolpe, Fitzgerald, Sis) and they weren’t as inviting as this one. The only downside of this edition is that it barely contains any footnotes and commentary. There’s a new annotated one, titled "The Canticle of the Birds" (2014) which uses the same translation and also includes over 200 illustrations, but it’s not cheap or easy to get.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14265919419333614068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_98567042" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/98567042" />
    <a title="Lindsay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/205963-lindsay"><img alt="Lindsay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600122504p2/205963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/205963-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/205963-lindsay?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/205963-lindsay?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/205963-lindsay?shelf=not-the-lower-48">not-the-lower-48</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/205963-lindsay?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer98567042" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14595997868936613368">I first became aware of <i>The Conference of the Birds</i> when reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54859.Lipstick_Jihad_A_Memoir_of_Growing_Up_Iranian_in_America_And_American_in_Iran' title='Lipstick Jihad A Memoir of Growing Up Iranian in America And American in Iran by Azadeh Moaveni' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lipstick Jihad: A Memoir of Growing Up Iranian in America And American in Iran</a> many years ago, which sparked a fascination with the tale's message. I, however, never got around to reading the whole thing--I stalled after a couple of attempts and later experienced it through Peter Sís' interpretation. But this year's Read Harder gave me the extra push to finally spend time with the thing.<br/><br/>Annnnd.... I wanted to like it more. To be fa</span>
  <span id="freeText14595997868936613368" style="display:none">I first became aware of <i>The Conference of the Birds</i> when reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54859.Lipstick_Jihad_A_Memoir_of_Growing_Up_Iranian_in_America_And_American_in_Iran" title="Lipstick Jihad A Memoir of Growing Up Iranian in America And American in Iran by Azadeh Moaveni" rel="nofollow noopener">Lipstick Jihad: A Memoir of Growing Up Iranian in America And American in Iran</a> many years ago, which sparked a fascination with the tale's message. I, however, never got around to reading the whole thing--I stalled after a couple of attempts and later experienced it through Peter Sís' interpretation. But this year's Read Harder gave me the extra push to finally spend time with the thing.<br /><br />Annnnd.... I wanted to like it more. To be fair, an epic poem told almost entirely in rhyming couplets was going to be a challenge, as I find nearly all rhyming poetry distracting. I have also been wooed by Sís' version, which is a bit more secular than the original, and restructures the action a little. I was unprepared to spend well over half the book just listening to the birds' excuses (OMG--I did have to take a break from the whiny birds), and while in theory I liked the idea of the Hoopooe following every directive with a handful of parables, it just got to wear on me. I'm glad I read it--don't get me wrong--and I still like the message, but I may stick to revisiting the Sís version from here on out (however sacrilege that may be). In fact, I should re-re-re*read that sucker again to compare.<br /><br />********<br />Read Harder: Poetry in translation with a theme other than love</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_782998582" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/782998582" />
    <a title="Troy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1851816-troy"><img alt="Troy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1231279778p2/1851816.jpg" /></a>
