      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Troy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Troy" href="/user/show/1851816-troy">Troy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1851816-troy?shelf=gave-up-on">gave-up-on</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1851816-troy?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer782998582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14644581805250054605">I'm reading about Christian pastoral governance which is all about the renunciation of one's will, and one's obedience and subservience to your superiors, and ultimately, to God. And that's what this long poem is all about. Renounce your ego; renounce your will; abandon your desires; and be obedient to those that command you. Ultimately, be fully obedient to God.<br/><br/>Now, there are some aspects of this I can get behind, but this poem's obsequious and lachrymose obsession with obedience bores the shit</span>
  <span id="freeText14644581805250054605" style="display:none">I'm reading about Christian pastoral governance which is all about the renunciation of one's will, and one's obedience and subservience to your superiors, and ultimately, to God. And that's what this long poem is all about. Renounce your ego; renounce your will; abandon your desires; and be obedient to those that command you. Ultimately, be fully obedient to God.<br /><br />Now, there are some aspects of this I can get behind, but this poem's obsequious and lachrymose obsession with obedience bores the shit out of me. <br /><br />Worse, the poem, which is about an assembly of birds who join in a quest to find a holy king bird, is boring. A quest shouldn't be boring! But the quest is a tiny part of the poem. Most of the book is filled with whining birds who make hobbits look vainglorious. The birds ceaselessly complain and bitch, and each bird represents a base desire that is countered with lessons from their leader about what it means to be good and true. And maybe it's the translation, but where is the beauty in the prose? <br /><br />Now let me state that I dislike the ideology that surrounds me. But fuck these birds. I'm off to another poem and another philosophy. I'm not interested in obedience or God or total renunciation of one's will.</span>

<div id="review_1753440208" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1753440208" />
    <a title="James Violand" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6308377-james-violand"><img alt="James Violand" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388703262p2/6308377.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1753440208?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Violand" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Violand" href="/user/show/6308377-james-violand">James Violand</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those interested in Sufi poetry.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6308377-james-violand?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1753440208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14059551385684365401">Attar was a wonderful poet, a confusing adherent of Islam and an extremely vain man who claimed Gabriel assisted him in writing this poem thereby equating himself with Mohammed. I cannot say for certain that he was a Sufi, nor that his "way" is unique. All of the great religions have mystics. What I can say from my experience in investigating these religions is that I believe this beautiful quest toward the Godhead is written with a very insulting and defensive position. It is as though a religi</span>
  <span id="freeText14059551385684365401" style="display:none">Attar was a wonderful poet, a confusing adherent of Islam and an extremely vain man who claimed Gabriel assisted him in writing this poem thereby equating himself with Mohammed. I cannot say for certain that he was a Sufi, nor that his "way" is unique. All of the great religions have mystics. What I can say from my experience in investigating these religions is that I believe this beautiful quest toward the Godhead is written with a very insulting and defensive position. It is as though a religion must denigrate all others to show its superiority. Some tales take a pathetic turn against those confessing their sins. And Attar brags about Omar burning down the Alexandria Library because it was filled with philosophy and contrary to the Koran. (This has been claimed as historical fact - but it isn't because it had ceased to exist long before Omar.) There is very little love shown one's fellow man, but he professes it toward God. The journey is an egoistic endeavor where one must ignore everything to pursue the journey. Instead of a guide to God in the guise of birds, I see ants climbing over one another in a struggle to be best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14059551385684365401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer9833866323294565">Written by the Persian mystical poet Farid Ud-Din Attar in the 12th century, this epic poem is an allegory for the philosophical and theological beliefs of Sufism. Many of the tales within this are applicable to everyone regardless of whether you are religious, spiritual or have secular beliefs. Some of these ideas include self-awareness, universality and the annihilation of the ego.<br/><br/>The translation itself is highly commendable as it maintains the rhyme whilst also bringing to life the captivatin</span>
  <span id="freeText9833866323294565" style="display:none">Written by the Persian mystical poet Farid Ud-Din Attar in the 12th century, this epic poem is an allegory for the philosophical and theological beliefs of Sufism. Many of the tales within this are applicable to everyone regardless of whether you are religious, spiritual or have secular beliefs. Some of these ideas include self-awareness, universality and the annihilation of the ego.<br /><br />The translation itself is highly commendable as it maintains the rhyme whilst also bringing to life the captivating imagery of the original. <br /><br />4.5 stars. Highly recommend.<br /><br />"The devil complains<br /><br />A sluggard once approached a fasting saint<br />And, baffled by despair, made this complaint:<br />"The devil is a highwayman, a thief,<br />Who's ruined me and robbed me of belief."<br />The saint replied: "Young man, the devil too<br />Has made his way here to complain - of you.<br />'My province is the world,' I heard him say;<br />'Tell this new pilgrim of God's holy Way<br />To keep his hands off what is mine - if I<br />Attack him it's because his fingers pry<br />In my affairs; if he will leave me be,<br />He's no concern of mine and can go free'." <br />(p. 99)</span>
  <a data-text-id="9833866323294565" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/341251547" />

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ivan Granger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ivan Granger" href="/user/show/9953914-ivan-granger">Ivan Granger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9953914-ivan-granger?shelf=muslim-sufi">muslim-sufi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9953914-ivan-granger?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer341251547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6219810907068921336">Attar’s masterpiece about a group of birds (human souls) under the leadership of a hoopoe (spiritual master) who determine to search for the legendary Simurgh (God). The birds must confront their own individual limitations and fears before they ultimately find the Simurgh and complete their quest. This translation is the best I’ve found — though I’m still waiting for a translation that transports me in the same way that FitzGerald’s translation of the Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam does.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Atiqah Ghazali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Atiqah Ghazali" href="/user/show/58139353-atiqah-ghazali">Atiqah Ghazali</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394730011" href="/book/show/2308015.CONFERENCE_OF_BIRDS">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58139353-atiqah-ghazali?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58139353-atiqah-ghazali?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3156443319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8344367678792378343">Musyawarah Burung<br/>(The Conference of The Birds)<br/>oleh Fariduddin Attar<br/>Published by Shambhala<br/>Diterbitkan oleh Kakatua<br/>Goodread's Rating: 4.23/5<br/>My Rating: 4.98/5<br/><br/>"Love is a cruel pain that devours everything.<br/>Sometimes it tears the veil from the soul, sometimes it draws it together.<br/>An atom of love is preferable to all that exists between the horizons, an atom of its pain better than the happy love of all lovers.<br/>Love is the marrow of beings; but there can be no real love without real suffering.<br/>Whoever is</span>
  <span id="freeText8344367678792378343" style="display:none">Musyawarah Burung<br />(The Conference of The Birds)<br />oleh Fariduddin Attar<br />Published by Shambhala<br />Diterbitkan oleh Kakatua<br />Goodread's Rating: 4.23/5<br />My Rating: 4.98/5<br /><br />"Love is a cruel pain that devours everything.<br />Sometimes it tears the veil from the soul, sometimes it draws it together.<br />An atom of love is preferable to all that exists between the horizons, an atom of its pain better than the happy love of all lovers.<br />Love is the marrow of beings; but there can be no real love without real suffering.<br />Whoever is grounded firm in love renounces faith, religion, and unbelief.<br />Love will open the door of spiritual poverty and poverty will show you the way of unbelief.<br />When there remains neither unbelief nor religion, your body and your soul will disappear; you will then be worthy of the mysteries- if you could fathom them, this is the only way."<br /><br />Never have I got haunted, consumed and obsessed with a book, I decided to read two translations back-to-back. This book taught, questioned and re-educate me about what I have known. It's like a pack of theology and adab studies, compact into less than 200 pages per book. If you are into Rumi and Hafez, this author, Fariduddin Attar or Attar of Nishapur is their teacher.<br /><br />"Seorang Sufi penggila Tuhan, diganggu anak-anak yang melemparinya dengan batu. Akhirnya ia pun berlindung di pojok sebuah gedung. Tetapi pada saat itu mulai turun hujan es, dan butir-butir es jatuh dari tingkat atap yang terbuka, menimpa kepala si gila itu.<br />Disangkanya butir-butir es itu kerikil-kerikil dan ia pun lalu menjulurkan lidahnya serta memaki anak-anak itu yang dibayangkannya sedang melemparkan kerikil-kerikil, karena rumah itu gelap. Akhirnya diketahuinya bahwa kerikil-kerikil itu hanya batu-batu es kiranya, dan ia pun menyesal dan berdoa, "O Tuhan, adalah karena rumah ini gelap maka aku telah berdosa dengan lidahku." <br />Bila kau memahami dasar-dasar perbuatan mereka yang ada dalam kegelapan, kau pun tentu akan memaafkan mereka."</span>
  <a data-text-id="8344367678792378343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText263426529872022669" style="display:none">Inspiration for Oscar Wilde's The Nightingale and the Rose:<br /><br />The Nightingale<br /><br />The nightingale raises his head, drugged with passion,<br />Pouring the oil of earthly love in such a fashion<br />That the other birds shaded with his song, grow mute.<br />The leaping mysteries of his melodies are acute.<br />'I know the secrets of Love, I am their piper,'<br />He sings, 'I seek a David with broken heart to decipher<br />Their plaintive barbs, I inspire the yearning flute,<br />The daemon of the plucked conversation of the lute.<br />The roses are dissolved into fragrance by my song,<br />Hearts are torn with its sobbing tone, broken along<br />The fault lines of longing filled with desire's wrong.<br />My music is like the sky's black ocean, I steal<br />The listener's reason, the world becomes the seal<br />Of dreams for chosen lovers, where only the rose<br />Is certain. I cannot go further, I am lame, and expose<br />My anchored soul to the divine Way.<br />My love for the rose is sufficient, I shall stay<br />In the vicinity of its petalled image, I need<br />No more, it blooms for me the rose, my seed.<br /><br />The hoopoe replies: 'You love the rose without thought.<br />Nightingale, your foolish song is caught<br />By the rose's thorns, it is a passing thing.<br />Velvet petal, perfume's repose bring<br />You pleasure, yes, but sorrow too<br />For the rose's beauty is shallow: few<br />Escape winter's frost. To seek the Way<br />Release yourself from this love that lasts a day.<br />The bud nurtures its own demise as day nurtures night.<br />Groom yourself, pluck the deadly rose from your sight."<br /><br />- from The Conference of the Birds, by Farid ud-Din Attar (trans. Raficq Abdull)</span>

<div id="review_821685067" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/821685067" />
    <a title="Simin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10874745-simin"><img alt="Simin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382184336p2/10874745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/821685067?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simin" href="/user/show/10874745-simin">Simin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444346" href="/book/show/144870.The_Conference_of_the_Birds">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer821685067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11304374968962281638">This is the second book of Persian classical poetry book that I have read in its entirety. I used the online edition “Ganjour”, which contains the works of many Persian poets, both classical and contemporary. Ganjour provides access to the Persian dictionary by Dehkhoda, so looking up the definition of unfamiliar words was not a burden. I also listened to the audio edition of the book, narrated by Farid Hamed.<br/><br/>The Conference of the Birds is an introduction to Sufism.<br/>Since my knowledge of Sufism i</span>
  <span id="freeText11304374968962281638" style="display:none">This is the second book of Persian classical poetry book that I have read in its entirety. I used the online edition “Ganjour”, which contains the works of many Persian poets, both classical and contemporary. Ganjour provides access to the Persian dictionary by Dehkhoda, so looking up the definition of unfamiliar words was not a burden. I also listened to the audio edition of the book, narrated by Farid Hamed.<br /><br />The Conference of the Birds is an introduction to Sufism.<br />Since my knowledge of Sufism is extremely limited (ok… nonexistent!) I used 2 other sources: the lectures by the late Prof. Mahammad Jafar Mahjoub about Attar, and the English translation of the poem by Dick Davis. <br /><br />The book has a religious tone: Davis compares it to Dante’s Divine Comedy, in which both works describe religious belief in beautiful poetry. The prelude section is particularly heavy in that respect, and it was my least favorite part of the book.<br /><br />Although I believe I need much more reading about Sufism to comprehend the so many allegories in the book, I really enjoyed the beautiful poetry, especially when the birds speak!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11304374968962281638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15688063662592215634">I read two versions of this book, the first one was a more English version It was translated, well I thought I'm not a linguist. This looks like the poetic version which i read second. I'll have to go through my files of notes, more like scraps of paper. Oh well ... This poetic version transformed time or simply put It made me stop and just wonder, I really enjoyed this book in either version. I do not always do poetry well, but this book like reading Dante shows how beautiful poetry can be even</span>
  <span id="freeText15688063662592215634" style="display:none">I read two versions of this book, the first one was a more English version It was translated, well I thought I'm not a linguist. This looks like the poetic version which i read second. I'll have to go through my files of notes, more like scraps of paper. Oh well ... This poetic version transformed time or simply put It made me stop and just wonder, I really enjoyed this book in either version. I do not always do poetry well, but this book like reading Dante shows how beautiful poetry can be even if it makes you stop and wonder It is well worth your time. Some stories are fun some are sublime. And other stories just are stunning.<br /><br /><br /><br />I am looking forward to re reading this book under the isbn # 0877730318 as this version is translated by C.S. Nott.... Soon this book will arive....Hello </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer600041488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14265337235633664511">This is the first work of Persian or Islamic poetry that I have ever read, and I must say I loved it! The main story-line about the birds was very entertaining and insightful. In addition to the main story of the birds, the additional tales and illustrative stories also were amusing and thought-provoking. <br/>A quick note on the edition of the book. The edition I read was an English translation of the original(Penguin Classics, paperback, 1984). One good thing about this English edition was that the</span>
  <span id="freeText14265337235633664511" style="display:none">This is the first work of Persian or Islamic poetry that I have ever read, and I must say I loved it! The main story-line about the birds was very entertaining and insightful. In addition to the main story of the birds, the additional tales and illustrative stories also were amusing and thought-provoking. <br />A quick note on the edition of the book. The edition I read was an English translation of the original(Penguin Classics, paperback, 1984). One good thing about this English edition was that the rhyme was kept in the translation. I personally felt that this helped the flow of the tale and made for a more entertaining read.<br />Finally, I would like to apologize for the multiple uses of "I" in this review. My journey to end "the Self" has just begun. :3</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VSa/nJX6ZbMOpN9lPA+QoipEIMFaRWbnJ6xCzW1pznJ9KYlH5BNpZzEKV+Zg5Wwl+wPX2se/PknbqEQC0T6DrA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_157985'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/157985.Shahnameh?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Shahnameh: The Persian Book of Kings<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/6584435.Abolqasem_Ferdowsi?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Abolqasem Ferdowsi<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><\/span> 4.52 avg rating &mdash; 4,154 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1010\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer12869141463374579481\">Among the great works of world literature, perhaps one of the least familiar to English readers is the \"Shahnameh: ThePersian Book of Kings,\" the national epic of Persia. This prodigious narrative, composed by the poet Ferdowsi between the years 980 <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12869141463374579481\" style=\"display:none\">Among the great works of world literature, perhaps one of the least familiar to English readers is the &quot;Shahnameh: ThePersian Book of Kings,&quot; the national epic of Persia. This prodigious narrative, composed by the poet Ferdowsi between the years 980 and 1010, tells the story of pre- Islamic Iran, beginning in the mythic time of Creation and continuing forward to the Arab invasion in the seventh century. As a window on the world, &quot;Shahnameh&quot; belongs in the company of such literary masterpieces as Dante\'s &quot;Divine Comedy,&quot; the plays of Shakespeare, the epics of Homer- classics whose reach and range bring whole cultures into view. In its pages are unforgettable moments of national triumph and failure, human courage and cruelty, blissful love and bitter grief.\n\nIn tracing the roots of Iran, &quot;Shahnameh&quot; initially draws on the depths of legend and then carries its story into historical times, when ancient Persia was swept into an expanding Islamic empire. Now Dick Davis, the greatest modern translator of Persian poetry, has revisited that poem, turning the finest stories of Ferdowsi\'s original into an elegant combination of prose and verse. For the first time in English, in the most complete form possible, readers can experience &quot;Shahnameh&quot; in the same way that Iranian storytellers have lovingly conveyed it in Persian for the past thousand years.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12869141463374579481\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div The state of God-Realisation is symbolised through union with a Beloved, and drinking the wine of spiritual love.\n\nThis compact version of the Divan of Hafez is a facsimile illuminated manuscript, complete with beautiful Persian calligraphy and miniature illustrations. There are 43 ghazals, translated into English by classical scholar Gertrude Bell. It is a truly beautiful introduction not only to the works of this beloved Sufi mystic, but also to the artistry of Mahmoud Farshchian. It is like getting two books in one: poetry and art.\n\n&quot;Hafiz has no peer.&quot; — Goethe\nPoetry is the greatest literary form of ancient Persia and modern Iran, and the fourteenth-century poet known as Hafiz is its preeminent master. Little is known about the poet\'s life, and there are more legends than facts relating to the particulars of his existence. This mythic quality is entirely appropriate for the man known as &quot;The Interpreter of Mysteries&quot; and &quot;The Tongue of the Hidden,&quot; whose verse is regarded as oracular by those seeking guidance and attempting to realize wishes.\nA mere fraction of what is presumed to have been an extensive body of work survives. This collection is derived from Hafiz\'s Divan (collected poems), a classic of Sufism. The short poems, called ghazals, are sonnet-like arrangements of varied numbers of couplets. In the tradition of Persian poetry and Sufi philosophy, each poem corresponds to two interpretations, sensual and mystic.\nThis outstanding translation of Hafiz\'s poetry was created by historian and Barks\'s translations capture the inward exploration and intensity that characterize Rumi\'s poetry, making this unique voice of mysticism and desire contemporary while remaining true to the original poems. In this volume readers will encounter the essence of Sufism\'s insights into the experience of divine love, wisdom, and the nature of both humanity and God.\n\nWhile Barks\'s stamp on this collection is clear, it is Rumi\'s voice that leaps off these pages with a rapturous power that leaves readers breathless. These poems express our deepest yearning for the transcendent connection with the source of the divine: there are passionate outbursts about the torment of longing for the beloved and the sweet delight that comes from union; stories of sexual adventures and of loss; poems of love and fury, sadness and joy; and quiet truths about the beauty and variety of human emotion. For Rumi, soul and body and emotion are not separate but are rather part of the great mystery of mortal life, a riddle whose solution is love. Above all else, Rumi\'s poetry exposes us to the delight that comes from being fully alive, urging us always to put aside our fears and take the risk of discovering our core self:\n\nNo one knows what makes the soul wake up so happy! Maybe a dawn breeze has blown the veil from the face of God. These fresh, original translations magnificently convey Rumi\'s insights into the human heart and its longings with his signature passion and daring, focusing on the ecstatic experience of the inseparability of human and divine love. Through annotated illustrations, journals, maps, and dreamscapes, Peter Sí­s shows what life was like for a child who loved to draw, proudly wore the red scarf of a Young Pioneer, stood guard at the giant statue of Stalin, and believed whatever he was told to believe. But adolescence brought questions. Cracks began to appear in the Iron Curtain, and news from the West slowly filtered into the country. Sí­s learned about beat poetry, rock \'n\' roll, blue jeans, and Coca-Cola. He let his hair grow long, secretly read banned books, and joined a rock band. Then came the Prague Spring of 1968, and for a teenager who wanted to see the world and meet the Beatles, this was a magical time. It was short-lived, however, brought to a sudden and brutal end by the Soviet-led invasion. But this brief flowering had provided a glimpse of new possibilities--creativity could be discouraged but not easily killed.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7457124.Attar_of_Nishapur">About Attar of Nishapur</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7457124.Attar_of_Nishapur"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1539433646p3/7457124.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7457124.Attar_of_Nishapur">Attar of Nishapur
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
202
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9515365385349898040">Abū amīd bin Abū Bakr Ibrāhīm (c. 1145 – c. 1221; Persian: ابو حامد بن ابوبکر ابراهیم‎), better known by his pen-names Farīd ud-Dīn (فرید الدین) and Attār (عطار, "the perfumer"), was a Persian Muslim poet, theoretician of Sufism, and hagiographer from Nishapur who had an immense and lasting influence on Persian poetry and Sufism.</span>

</div>
</div>
